<div class="word-content">
<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;
margin-bottom:0in;margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:center'><b><span
style='font-size:14.0pt'>THE NEW TESTAMENT’S WITNESS TO THE </span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;
margin-bottom:0in;margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:center'><b><span
style='font-size:14.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;
margin-bottom:0in;margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:center'><b><span
style='font-size:14.0pt'>OLD TESTAMENT’S INSPIRATION*</span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;
margin-bottom:0in;margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:center'><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;
margin-bottom:0in;margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:center'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>Virgil Warren, PhD</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;
margin-bottom:0in;margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:center'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;
margin-bottom:0in;margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:center'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>I. Significant
Silence   </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            The New Testament raises no objections to
the Jewish canon as we have it today in thirty-nine books. That observation
holds despite the differing ways the Jews counted the books, despite the
religious and geographical divisions among the Jews, and despite additional Jewish
books that grew up between the testaments, especially the Apocrypha.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            A.  The first-century canon</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  1.   Methods of enumeration</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.   <u>Twenty-two</u>. Palestinian
and Alexandrian Jews counted twenty-two sacred writings (equal to the
twenty-two letters of the Hebrew alphabet). They obtained that number by
grouping the twelve minor prophets together<span style='position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'>1</span> and by regarding Ezra and Nehemiah, 1 and 2 Samuel, 1 and
2 Kings, and 1 and 2 Chronicles respectively as units. Judges and Ruth, and
Jeremiah and Lamentations were counted together as one book.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.   <u>Twenty-four</u>. 4 (2)
Esdras <b>14</b>:45 (A.D. 85-96) gives twenty-four (the number of letters in
the Greek alphabet). The differing totals of twenty-two and twenty-four came
from whether Ruth and Lamentations were attached to Judges and Jeremiah or
listed separately.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        c.   <u>Twenty-seven</u>. At a
later time, the number twenty-seven was used occasionally (corresponding to the
twenty-two Hebrew letters plus the five letters with terminal forms). In this
numbering, 1 and 2 Samuel, 1 and 2 Kings, and 1 and 2 Chronicles became six
volumes.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  2.   Kinds of classification</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.   <u>Unitary</u>. Several
classifications of the Jewish scriptures were in use during the ministry of
Christ. General terms such as <i>the Law</i>,<span style='position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'>2 </span><i>hallowed writings</i>,<span style='position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'>3 </span>and <i>holy scriptures</i><span style='position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'>4 </span>grouped all the books together.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.  <u>Bipartite</u>. A
two-part division of the Old Testament was indicated in the expressions <i>Moses
and the prophets</i><span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>5</span> and <i>the
Law and the prophets</i>.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>6</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        c.   <u>Tripartite</u>. A more
formal classification was the tripartite division of <i>Torah</i> (Law), <i>Nebi’im</i>
(Prophets), and <i>Ketubim</i> (Writings, or Hagiographa), as in the <i>Prologue
to Ecclesiasticus</i> (<i>cir</i>. 132 B.C.).<span style='position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'> 7</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>                        </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>d.   “<u>Four-Pentateuch</u>.” Sometimes the
twenty-two books were divided into four “Pentateuchs”: (1) the five books of
Moses; (2) five books of history: Joshua, Judges, Samuel, Kings, and Chronicles;
(3) five books of poetry: Job, Psalms, Proverbs, Ecclesiastes, and Song of
Solomon; and (4) five books of prophecy: Isaiah, Jeremiah, Ezekiel, Daniel, and
the Minor Prophets. Ezra, Nehemiah, and Esther were counted separately.<span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>8</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  3.   Practices of classifying</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.   <u>Affected by numbering
of the books</u>. In the tripartite division, Jewish writers were not always
consistent where to books like Ruth and Lamentations. In the number twenty-two,
Ruth and Lamentations were among the <i>Nebi’im</i> as attachments to Judges
and Jeremiah, and so were in the Prophets. Under the twenty-four count system,
they were among the <i>Ketubim</i> and separate from Judges and Jeremiah.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              In his <i>Contra Apionem</i>
1:8 (<i>cir</i>. A.D. 100), Josephus adopts another form of the tripartite
system, cataloguing the books historically: (1) five books of Moses; (2)
thirteen books of the prophets; and (3) four books of hymns and wise counsels.
The first class equals the Pentateuch; the last group must be Psalms, Proverbs,
Ecclesiastes, and Song of Solomon, the rest of the books grouped with the
Prophets.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.   <u>Affected by the
categorizing of the books</u>. Other variations were caused by the number of
categories into which the twenty-two books were divided. Ezra, Nehemiah, and
Est              her were not classified in the “four-Pentateuch” system,
Josephus listed them among the prophets, and they were in the <i>Kethubhim</i>
in the common tripartite division. In the “four-Pentateuch” system 1 and 2
Chronicles were called history; by Josephus they were listed in the prophets;
under the threefold division they were in the <i>Kethubhim</i>. Joshua, Judges,
1 and 2 Samuel, and 1 and 2 Kings were among the prophets except in the
four-division list, which made them history. The Book of Daniel was counted
with the prophets except in the tripartite division, which numbered it among
the writings. Nevertheless, changes of classification and enumeration did not
affect the actual contents of the first-century canon.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  4.  Religious divisions</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.   <u>Samaritans</u>. Having
a common Jewish canon has been questioned in light </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>of the Samaritans’ use of only the Pentateuch.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.   <u>Sadducees</u>. Special
attention has been fixed on the Sadducees because some of the early Christian
fathers have reported that the Sadducees, too, accepted only the Pentateuch, a
mistake which William Henry Green conjectures was caused by confusing
Samaritans and Sadducees.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>9</span> Regardless
of the merit of his suggestion, it is true that no other direct evidence
attests the Sadducees’ rejection of the Prophets and Hagiographa.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              In <i>Antiquities</i>
Josephus has passages (<b>13</b>:10:16; <b>18</b>:1:4) sometimes taken to mean
that the Sadducees accepted only the Pentateuch, but the objection in those
citations is to the traditions of the elders. In Matthew<b> 22</b>:23-32 Jesus’
reference to Exodus <b>3</b>:6 in his defense of the doctrine of resurrection
need not imply their rejection of everything but the Pentateuch. He may have
sought to show that the doctrine of resurrection pervaded the scriptures from
earliest times. A reference from Moses’ writings would have been of special
force because God used him to establish the whole Old Testament economy. In
addition, the passage Jesus quoted may have given him an advantage in debate
because it probably had not figured in previous arguments with Sadducees about
resurrection.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-31.5pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                                         Their denial
of resurrection (Acts <b>23</b>:8) does not prove the rejection of those scriptures
that speak of it (<i>e.g</i>., Daniel <b>12</b>:2), any more than disbelief in
angels proves their rejection of the Pentateuch (Mark <b>12</b>:18).<span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>10</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              The Talmud mentions a
Sadducee that quoted the Book of Amos. Rabbi Gamaliel, arguing with a Sadducee
in Talmudic sources, uses Isaiah and the Song of Solomon. The Sadducee in reply
does not object to the books Gamaliel used, but argues for another
interpretation of the passages.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>11</span> The
other sects of the Jews leave no indication of a variant list of inspired
writings.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  5.  Geographical groupings</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.   <u>Alexandrian</u>. The
position is sometimes taken that Alexandrian Jews used a larger canon than the
ones in Palestine. The main argument is the presence of the Apocrypha in the
Septuagint, a Greek translation of the Old Testament made in Alexandria. Showing
various Apocryphal writings in the LXX depends on the manuscripts available to us.
None earlier than A.D. 350 have survived. So, a period of six hundred years lies
between those copies and the translating work during the reign of Ptolemy
Philadelphus (285-247 B.C.).<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>12</span> Over
such a long time the Apocrypha may have been inserted by other Hellenistic Jews
(or Christians), not necessarily as scripture but as Jewish religious writings.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              The best manuscripts of
the Septuagint do not contain the same extra books. Vaticanus (fourth century)
contains no Book of Maccabees. Sinaiticus (fourth century) omits Baruch, which
is deemed canonical by Catholics, but contains 4 Maccabees, which they reject. Alexandrinus
(fifth century) even has 3 and 4 Maccabees and 1 Esdras, not received by Rome. Those
manuscripts are uniform about canonical books. The addition of 1 and 2 Clement
in Alexandrinus, and <i>Barnabas</i> and <i>Hermas</i> in Sinaiticus are other
important examples that support the proposition that the canon, whether New
Testament or Old, cannot be constructed from the contents of manuscripts. Direct
testimony is the only reliable source.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              Philo (<i>cir</i>. 20
B.C. to A.D. 50), an eminent scholar and philosopher, is the only Alexandrian
Jew whose writings have come down from antiquity. He never cites the Apocryphal
books, though he repeatedly quotes them (even as inspired) and mentions every
one of the twenty-two Old Testament books except Esther, Ezekiel, Daniel,
Ecclesiastes, and the Song of Solomon.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              The fact that
Alexandrian Jews wanted to maintain good relations with Jews in the Holy Land
establishes a strong presumption against their using a different canon. Such a
deviation would have been unorthodox and divisive. Furthermore, according to <i>The
Letter of Aristeas</i>, it was Palestinian Jews that translated the LXX at Ptolemy’s
request.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>13</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>                        </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>b.  <u>Palestinian</u>. After giving the numerical
divisions of the twenty-two sacred books, Josephus in <i>Contra Apionem</i> <b>1</b>:8
says, </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-left:.75in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>“It is instinctive in all Jews at once from their
birth to regard them as commands of God, and to abide by them, and if need be, to
be willing to die for them.”</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>His statement not only indicates that the various Jewish
sects accepted one canon, but that “all Jews” everywhere in the world had the
same books, especially since Josephus is arguing against a Gentile assailant.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        c.  <u>In comparison</u>. Josephus
prefaces the above remarks with a statement about the respect Jews had for
their scriptures:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>      </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-left:.75in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <i>“How firmly we have given credit to
these books of our own nation, is evident by what we do: for during so many
ages as have already passed no one hath been so bold as either to add anything
to them, to take anything from them, or to make any change in them.”</i><span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>14</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>The words of Philo the Alexandrian are comparable<i>: “They
have not changed so much as a single word in them. They would rather die a
thousand deaths than detract anything from these laws and statutes.”</i><span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>15</span> Such reverence for the
twenty-two books is incompatible with an enlarged canon.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              The prologue of
Ecclesiasticus uses the terms <i>law</i>, <i>prophets</i>, and <i>the rest of
the books</i> when speaking of both Palestinian and Alexandrian scripture
without making any distinction between them. In the second century A.D., the
Alexandrian Jews adopted Aquila’s Greek version of the Old Testament, and
Aquila’s version excluded the Apocrypha. Origen, a Christian in Alexandria a
little later, gave no approval of the Apocrypha. Of more direct importance on
the original contents of the LXX is the statement of Cyril of Jerusalem: <i>“Read
the divine Scriptures, namely the twenty-two books of the Old Testament, which
the seventy-two interpreters translated.”</i><span style='position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'>16</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  6.  Rabbinic disputations</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.   <u>Confirmatory of an
established canon</u>. Another matter regarding the identity of the
first-century canon concerns the objections raised by Jewish rabbis against
certain books of the Hebrew canon, particularly Esther, Ecclesiastes, and Song
of Solomon.  Ezekiel and Proverbs also came in question occasionally. Those
disputations were not over admitting any book into the canon, but over
retaining it there. So, the questions were directed toward a canon already in
existence.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b. <u>Individual objections</u>.
Those exceptions were raised by individual rabbis, and merely emphasize the
virtual unanimity of the Jewish people on this matter. Popular opinion had long
been settled, and the “official” decisions left the matter as they found it.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  7.  New Testament data</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.  <u>Terminology relevant to
a literary unit</u>. The New Testament itself testifies to the Old Testament as
a literary unit. In Acts <b>8</b>:32, Luke says of the Ethiopian eunuch, <i>“Now
the passage of scripture he was reading was this . . . .”</i> The singular <u>scripture</u>
speaks of a body of literature that contained the passage.<span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>17</span> <i>Scriptures</i> also refers to
a set canon when Luke records that Paul entered the synagogue at Thessalonica <i>“and
for three Sabbath days reasoned with them from the scriptures.”</i><span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>18</span> The definite article specifies a
particular group of writings.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>19</span> Paul
introduces his Roman epistle saying that <i>“the gospel of God”</i> was <i>“promised
before through his prophets in the holy scriptures” </i>(Romans <b>1</b>:20). <i>The
Law</i>, though used variously, often refers to the entire Old Testament: <i>“In
the Law it is written, ‘By men of strange languages and by the lips of foreigners
I will speak to this people; and not even that way will they listen to me, says
Lord says.’”</i><span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>20</span> The
quotation is from Isaiah <b>28</b>:11, not the Pentateuch. In 2 Timothy <b>3</b>:15,
Paul commends the young evangelist for knowing from a baby <i>“the sacred
writings that can make you wise to salvation through faith in Christ Jesus.”</i> 
Jesus told the Pharisees, <i>“The Law and the prophets were until John; from
that time the gospel of the kingdom of God is preached.”</i><span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>21</span> An expression similar to <i>Law
and prophets</i> occurs in Luke <b>16</b>:29: <i>“They have Moses and the
prophets; let them hear them”</i> (cp. <b>16</b>:31).<span style='position:
relative;top:-3.0pt'> </span>Jesus tells his disciples after his resurrection,</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-left:.75in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>“These are the words that I spoke to you while I was still
with you, that everything must be fulfilled that are written in the Law of
Moses, the prophets, and the Psalms about me”</span></i><span style='font-size:
12.0pt'> [Luke <b>24</b>:44].</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-67.5pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>                             </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>All these terms testify to a literary unit recognized
without definition.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.  <i> “<u>From Abel to
Zachariah</u>.”</i> Of interest in this connection is the statement </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              of Jesus in Matthew <b>23</b>:35:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-left:.75in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>“That upon you may come all the righteous blood shed
on the earth from the blood of Abel the righteous unto the blood of Zachariah
son of Barachiah, whom you killed between the sanctuary and the altar.”</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>The reference is most probably to Zechariah (variant
spelling of Zachariah), who was stoned at the order of King Joash <i>“in the
court of the house of Yahveh” </i>in the ninth century B.C.<span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>22 </span><i>All the righteous blood</i>
includes at least the martyrdoms recorded in scripture. Jesus seems to have
been thinking of the Old Testament in the literary sense, because Zechariah was
not the last martyr chronologically speaking. In the seventh century Uriah
ben-Shemiah was murdered with the sword during the reign of Jehoiakim, son of
Josiah, king of Judah. Christ’s statement seems to presuppose a canon whose
books were arranged in a fashion similar to that used in the modern rabbinic
Bible, where 2 Chronicles stands last, being the final book of the <i>Kethubhim</i>,
the third division of the Hebrew scripture under the Masoretic, tripartite
system. Jesus’ reference to Zechariah provides confirmatory evidence that the
Jews of his day possessed a distinct group of sacred writings.<span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>23</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  8.  Early Christian testimony</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.   <u>Inconsistent in
practice</u>. Certain early Christian writers used the Apocrypha, introducing
it at times inadvisedly with the formula <i>as the Holy Spirit says</i>.<span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>24</span> That practice has led some
critics to claim that books introduced that way were considered scripture. But
even as the contents of manuscripts are no criterion for canonicity, so also
the usage of uninspired men does not form a good basis for judging even their
own views of the canon, because conviction and practice do not always correspond.
Their own direct testimony found in formal listings and express approvals must provide
final judgment; they never approve apocryphal books in such lists.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.   <u>Actual belief</u>. An
indication of the attitude of early Christians toward apocryphal works is
reflected in the hesitancy of some to accept the Book of Jude. Clement of
Alexandria, Origen, and Didymus among others, assumed that Jude used the <i>Assumption
of Moses</i>, an apocryphal work no longer extant.<span style='position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'>25</span> Jerome mentions that those doubts had arisen because Jude
used apocryphal literature authoritatively.<span style='position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'>26</span> Whether Jude cites the <i>Assumption of Moses</i> and the
<i>Book of Enoch</i> or cites them authoritatively is not the point. The date
of writing for these apocryphal books is uncertain. Furthermore, Jude may be putting
his approval on a bit of authentic oral tradition rather than on a written
expression of it. The best approach supposes that Jude cited these works for
illustrative purposes. The point is that some early Christian writers, having
assumed that Jude cited these works authoritatively, could not harmonize using
apocryphal books with their view of inspiration: inspired men do not base their
message on uninspired sources.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>27</span> They
regarded the citing of apocryphal literature as a reason for rejection. That
test of inspiration they applied also to their own writings and considered
their denial of personal inspiration to be consistent with their own use of
apocryphal writings. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            B.  The argument from silence</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  1.  Silence of Jesus</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.   <u>In light of his
denunciation of the Jews for ignorance, false interpretation, and disobedience</u>.
The New Testament witnesses that the Old Testament is inspired since it records
no time when Jesus or any of his followers ever rejected the canon that their
fellow Jews accepted. That observation amounts to more than an argument from
silence; it is an argument from lesser to greater because Christ addressed
himself to lesser things than the correctness of the canon. He charged the Jews
with ignorance of the Law (Matthew <b>12</b>:7); he denounced their false
interpretations of it (Matthew <b>23</b>:1-33); he accused them of disobeying
it (Mark<b> 7</b>:8-13); and he condemned their traditions that made it void;
but he uttered not a word of censor for adding books that had no right to be in
it.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              Ignorance, false
interpretation, and disobedience presuppose a content known and understood, and
a normative standard of obedience. On the lips of the Son of God who came to
reveal, give understanding, and lead people to obey the Father, there is the
implication that the Law in whose name he came was from God, not from human
tradition.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.   <u>In light of his
objections to Jewish tradition</u>. The Jews had developed a labyrinth of oral
tradition that they added to the Law by rabbinic custom. Those glosses Jesus
publicly opposed because some of them conflicted with the scripture itself.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-left:.75in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>“You leave God’s commandment and hold fast to human
tradition.” He told them, “Full well do you reject God’s commandment you can
keep your tradition. Moses said, ‘Honor your father and your mother’; and, ‘Let
him that speaks evil of father or mother certainly die’: but you say, “If a man
says to his father or his mother, ‘What you might have been profited from me is
Corban (that is to say, Given to God)’; you no longer let him to do anything
for his father or his mother; making God’s word void by the tradition you have
delivered. You do lots of things like that”</span></i><span style='font-size:
12.0pt'> (Mark <b>7</b>:8-13).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.75in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>But Jesus’ objection to those traditions lay deeper
than the fact that they often contradicted God’s word: they were not God’s word;
they were heavy burdens grievous to bear, placed on people’s shoulders by
religious leaders who were not willing to keep their own rules (Matthew<b> 23</b>:3-4).
People have no right to contradict God’s commands; neither may they add imperatives
to his revelation. God legislates his own laws, and any addition to them
contradicts his silence.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              The presence of
uncanonical writings in the Jewish canon would have been a more serious error
than oral tradition; the false and the true would have had equal authority. The
Jews at the time of Jesus’ ministry did not account their traditions as equal
in authority to the Law. They considered them a separate set of regulations to
serve as a protective hedge around the Law. If Jesus was so concerned about those
human traditions, he would surely have viced concern over a faulty canon, one
that falsely claimed to be from God. If the Spirit saw fit to record Jesus’ objection
to traditions, we would expect some corrective note about the canon. In light
of his condemnation of ignorance, false interpretation, disobedience of the
scriptures, and his denunciation of human traditions, his silence on the canon is
significant.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  2.  Silence of the Jews</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.   <u>In light of their
major indictments</u>. Even as the New Testament contains no criticism from
Jesus about the Jewish Law, it reveals no opposition to him by the Jews for
rejecting any part of their sacred writings. Throughout his ministry the
Sanhedrin kept close watch on his actions and teachings. They were quick to cry
of blasphemy against him; they saw implications in his self-designation “the
Son of God” (John <b>10</b>:25-39). Caiaphas and the Pharisees plotted his
death on the grounds that his ministry would take away their leadership and
dissolve the Jewish state, which was under Roman control (John <b>11</b>:48-49).
Their holy temple would lose its significance under a system which taught that God
accepts those in any place who worship God in spirit and truth.<span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>28</span> Against Jesus and his followers
they even raised the objection that Jesus would change the customs which Moses
had delivered (cp. Acts <b>6</b>:14), but they never accused him of saying
those customs were not inspired. It was that combination of his claim to change
them and his defense of their divine origin that brought against him the fury
of the religious leaders.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.   <u>In light of their
trivial objections</u>. Commissioned observers continually objected to Jesus’
disregard for the tradition of the elders;<span style='position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'>29</span> yet he refused to accede to their criticisms (Luke <b>11</b>:45-52).
They censored his actions,<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>30</span> opposed
his ministry, heckled his teaching, but never did they accuse him of denying
the inspiration of any of their holy scriptures. In light of their opposition
to him on comparatively unimportant things, the silence of the Jews is
significant on this important issue: the inspiration of the Law.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-right:-.5in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>II. Logical Implication   </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            The New Testament bears implicit testimony
to the inspiration of the Old Testament when it witnesses to a unity in the
progressive revelation of the Bible, that Jesus and the apostles considered the
Old Testament as authoritative, that the power of predictive prophecy is
attributed to the Old Testament, and that the Old Testament is viewed as the
word of God.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            A.  Unity of the Bible</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  1.  Unity of the Old Testament</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a. <u>Circumstances of its
writing</u>. The New Testament regards the Old Testament as a literary unit. That,
coupled with the circumstances of its writing, implies its inspired unity. The
Hebrew scriptures came from more than forty men during thousand years, at home
and in exile, during peaceful prosperity and in ruthless military subjugation,
in times of religious apostasy as well as times when Yahveh was worshiped in
truth. These men prophesied in the face of death, not yielding to fear for
their personal welfare. A unified message under such different circumstances is
inconceivable without supernatural intervention, revelation, and direction in
the overall writing and in each part. In a real sense, the whole was written by
one Writer.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.   <u>Mechanical uses of the
Old Testament</u>. The unity of the Law is implied in phenomena related to
obvious quotations of the Old Testament, factual allusions, and verbal
reminiscences.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>31</span> In citation
forms, where specific authorship is not mentioned, authorship appears
insignificant; the sheer fact that <i>“it is recorded in scripture”</i> is
sufficient.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>32</span> Many such
references use the impersonal forms of <i>graph</i>ō: <i>“it is written.” </i>These
and nearly all the other impersonal citation formulas are in the perfect tense,
connoting linear or stative, instead of punctiliar action.<span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>33</span> The lasting truth and importance
of the citation are even more emphatic when periphrastic perfects are employed:
<i>“it has been written.”</i><span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>34</span> To
say “it is written,” without needing to specify “by whom,” is to say in one
word that the Old Testament is a divine unit, an authoritative whole, a writing
of continuing worth. Only a “library” of divine unity may be cited anonymously
as a basis of religious authority.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              The unity of the Old
Testament is implicit in the practice of paralleling similar passages from
several books. Paul in Romans <b>15</b>:9-12 assembles four quotations which
show <i>“that the Gentiles might glorify God for his mercy”</i>:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              <i>“I will praise you
among the Gentiles,</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              and sing to your name.”</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                                       Again he says,</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              “Rejoice, you Gentiles,
with his people.”</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                                       Again,</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              “Praise the Lord, all
you Gentiles;</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              and let all the peoples
praise him.”</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                                       Again, Isaiah
says,</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              “There will be a root of
Jesse</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              that arises to rule the
Gentiles; </span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              the Gentiles hope on him.”</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>In these quotations, the apostle uses passages from
all three sections of the Hebrew scriptures: the second quotation is from the <i>Torah</i>
(Deuteronomy <b>32</b>:43); the first and third from the <i>Kethubhim</i>
(Psalm <b>18</b>:49 and Psalm<b> 117</b>:1); and the fourth from the <i>Nebi’im</i>
(Isaiah <b>11</b>:10). Together they provide a fourfold foundation for a
worldwide faith. Paul’s combining them presupposes the unity of the Old
Testament that faith has been built on.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              In Hebrews <b>1</b>:5-14
the author combines seven scriptures on the claim that the Son is superior to
angels. The quotations come from Deuteronomy, 2 Samuel, and five Psalms. His
purpose is to emphasize, which is possible only if writer and readers agreed on
the authoritative unity of the Old Testament.<span style='position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'>35</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              Several times in quoting
passages from the Old Testament, one citation formula introduces more than one
citation. Matthew <b>21</b>:5 uses the rubric <i>Now this is come to pass that what
the prophet might be fulfilled</i>. Then he quotes Isaiah <b>62</b>:11 and
Zechariah <b>9</b>:9 as if they were one scripture. Mark introduces his account
of John’s ministry with a combined quotation from Malachi and Isaiah, although
his citation formula mentions only Isaiah. In 2 Corinthians <b>6</b>:16-18 Paul
runs together verbiage from Leviticus, Exodus, Ezekiel, Jeremiah, Isaiah, and
Hosea with the expression <i>as God says</i>. Such a practice makes evident the
writer’s view that the Old Testament books are united because they were spoken
by God.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              Jesus’ threefold breakdown
of the Old Testament (Luke <b>24</b>:44) into law, prophets, and psalms, is
most likely another way of saying <i>Torah</i><u>,</u> <i>Nebi’im</i>, and <i>Ketubim</i>.
If the Book of Psalms stands metonymically for the Hagiographa, there is strong
implication for the unity of the Writings, an implication applicable to the
other scriptures (<b>24</b>:45), from which Jesus taught concerning the
fulfillment of prophecies relating to himself.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              By the time of Christ’s
ministry, the individual psalms had become a collection spoken of as a book.<span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>36</span> Written as they were by many
authors from Moses on for nearly a thousand years,<span style='position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'>37</span> their unity as one book epitomizes the unity of the Old
Testament.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              In Hebrews the author
uses the Old Testament in a striking way as he argues that a Sabbath rest remains
for God’s people. After paraphrasing Genesis <b>2</b>:2, he concludes from
greater to lesser that if God, the Creator of the universe, rested from his
work of creation, man, whom he created, may rest when he finishes his own work
within that creation (Hebrews <b>5</b>:3-10). To that major premise he adds a
minor premise from Psalm <b>95</b> that Joshua, in leading the Israelites into
Canaan, did not fulfill the human rest implicit in the divine rest. To that
historical and revelatory enthymeme, the author supplies the conclusion that
there yet remains a rest for faithful people.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              The writing of Genesis <b>2</b>:2
and Psalm <b>95</b>:11 was separated by “so long time” of four hundred years. The
possibility of constructing from scripture an enthymeme under such
circumstances implies the divine origin and unity of all the Old Testament,
because a truth claim must inhere in scripture <i>per se</i>. Not only so, but
what the writer by the Spirit infers from the divine rest (that there is also a
human rest) is itself directly asserted in Psalm <b>95</b>:11, which he also
quotes.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>38 </span>So, there comes
into view an accordance between the content of a logical inference from one
passage and the direct affirmation of another: logical validity resides in the
divine truth of the Old Testament.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>39</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              Factual allusions to the
Old Testament illustrate the unity of the Jewish economy and divine
intervention into the events and the record of them. That God had a hand in those
events could be known only by men in contact with him. Stephen was accused
before the Sanhedrin of teaching that Jesus would destroy the temple and change
the customs Moses had delivered. In his defense Stephen summarized the first
half of Hebrew history, choosing events that emphasized the divine origin of
the Mosaic Law, as well as the origin and purpose of the temple. In lining
himself up with Moses, he denied any opposition to his Law and showed that
they, not he, were opposing the Law of Moses. It was not so much that <u>Moses</u>
had given the <u>Israelites</u> the Law, but that <u>God</u> had given <u>Moses</u>
the Law, the same Moses who prophesied the coming of “the Righteous One” when
he declared that God would raise up to them from among their brethren one like
himself. The Jewish people could take no pride in their heritage, because they
resisted Moses and the Law God gave him for them, even as they were now
resisting “the Righteous One” Moses prophesied. As a people they were <i>“uncircumcised
in heart and ears”</i> despite <i>“the circumcision of the flesh”</i> that God
had given their ancestor Abraham as a promise of a progeny to inherit the Holy
Land and as a token of their covenant relationship with God. That relationship
they and their forefathers had broken by persecuting the prophets God had sent
to lead them to Christ and by refusing the Moses to whom they gave lipservice
as a ruler and judge over them. What they asserted in their desire to protect
his customs by persecuting Christ’s followers, they denied by disobeying his
command to hearken to his “like-me.” The history of their people should have
forewarned them against hastily rejecting one who came with such power as Jesus
demonstrated in proof of his Messianic claim, because that history had a
Messianic teleology. The history and its future significance were contained
only in the Old Testament, and the records themselves were a part of the very
program they recorded. As God’s hand was in the history, so was his influence
in the records that preserved its purpose.<span style='position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'>40</span>  </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              Verbal parallels of the
Old Testament writings occur frequently in the New Testament. Numerous examples
occur where a writer combines in one statement the expressions of two or more
books. In cleansing the temple, Jesus declares, <i>“It is written, ‘My house will
be called a house of prayer’; but you are making it a den of robbers.”</i> The
first clause is a quotation from Isaiah <b>56</b>:7. The second is a
condemnation cast in the wording of Jeremiah<b> 7</b>:11: <i>“Has this house
that is called by my name become a den of robbers in your eyes?”</i> It is inconceivable
that Jesus did not have Jeremiah’s statement in mind when he made his own. He
equated the authority of the two sources he used.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              Peter’s sermon on
Pentecost contains quotes and verbal parallels of Psalms, Isaiah, and Joel. His
summary statement in Acts <b>2</b>:39 draws from Isaiah and Joel: <i>“Because
the promise is to you and your children and all that are afar off, even as many
as the Lord our God will call to him.”</i> His words reflect Isaiah <b>57</b>:19:
<i>“Peace, peace, to the one that is far off and the one that is near”</i> and
Joel <b>2</b>:21: <i>“There will be those that escape . . . among the remnant .
. . that Yahveh calls.” </i>Whether deliberate or unconscious, the wording
betrays the belief that we can site the Old Testament at random because it is a
contextual whole.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  The “Magnificat,” “Benedictus,” and
the “Nunc Dimittis” of Luke <b>1-2</b> are prime examples of interweaving Old
Testament imagery and expression. In the scope of ten verses, Mary’s “Magnificat”
draws twice from 1 Samuel and Job, once from Psalm <b>41</b>, <b>89</b>, <b>103</b>,
<b>107</b>, <b>111</b>; Isaiah, and Micah (Luke <b>1</b>:46-55). Zacharias’
twelve verses reflect Psalm <b>41</b>, <b>105</b>, <b>106</b>, <b>111</b>, <b>132</b>,
Isaiah, and Malachi (Luke <b>1</b>:68-70). In the brief blessing the aging
Simon pronounced on the infant Jesus, he used Isaiah three times in four verses
(Luke <b>2</b>:29-32).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  The Book of Revelation contains no
formal quotation of an Old Testament book. As New Testament apocalypse, the
book looks forward prophetically rather than backward historically. Nevertheless,
it breathes the atmosphere of Old Testament prophetic writings, whose nature it
shares. Westcott and Hort in their Greek New Testament use uncial letters for
quotations and verbal parallels of Old Testament passages. Moulton collaborated
with these textual critics in formulating the list on which the uncial passages
are based. In the complete listing, given as an appendix to the Greek text,
there are 1078 quotations and reminiscences of the Old Testament in the New.<span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>41</span> Of that total, 243 appear in
Revelation. Its snatches of Old Testament verbiage follow and overlap one
another with such unintentional ease that formal analysis is nearly impossible
at times. In <b>15</b>:3, for example, John uses wording found in Exodus,
Deuteronomy, Joshua, Psalm <b>111</b>, Jeremiah, and Amos. In <b>19</b>:6 he
picks up expressions from Psalm <b>93</b>, <b>104</b>, Ezekiel, Daniel, and
Amos. Such unstudied combinations from various points in the Old Testament are
powerful proof of the unity of the thirty-nine books, because unity is assumed
as a settled matter, rather than one that needs demonstration.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  2.  Unity of the Old Testament and
the New</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.   <u>Harmony of the New and
the Old</u>. The possibility of believing both the Old Testament and the
Messiah points up the unity of their message: <i>“When he rose from the dead,
his disciples remembered he said that; and they believed the scripture and what
Jesus had said”</i> (John<b> 2</b>:22). The continuity of the Law and the
ministry of Christ is also implied in Paul’s sermon at Antioch of Pisidia when
he declares that the rulers of Jerusalem rejected Christ because they did not <i>“know
the prophets”</i> (Acts <b>13</b>:27). Christ himself said in a debate with the
Pharisees, <i>“You search the scriptures because you think that in them you
have life; and these are they which testify of me.”</i><span style='position:
relative;top:-3.0pt'>42</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.    <u>New Testament message
based on the Old Testament</u>. The inspiration of the Old Testament is implied
in the unity that the New Testament ascribes to it. Its inspiration follows from
the New Testament writers’ claims to inspiration together with their contention
that the message they preached grew out of the former revelation. Paul claims
inspiration in Galatians <b>1</b>:11-12:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-left:1.0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>“I make known to you, brothers, regarding the gospel
that I preached to you, that it is not after man. For neither did I receive it
from man, nor was I taught it, but it came to me through revelation of Jesus
Christ.”</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>43</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>In the same epistle (<b>3</b>:23) he bases the
Christian gospel on the Mosaic Law when he declares that the Law was a “tutor”
to prepare people for Christ.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>44</span> It
was the literary “voice in the wilderness,” added to the original Abrahamic
promise to detect, repress, and increase the awareness of sin. In that the Law served
as an introduction to an inspired message, the Law itself was inspired.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        c.  <u>New Testament
fulfillment of Old Testament prophecy</u>. The whole field of prophecy-fulfillment
assumes the unity of the prophecy and its New Testament fulfillment. That
assumption underlies the citation of Isaiah <b>7</b>:14 as a prediction of the
virgin birth, of Micah <b>5</b>:2 in connection with Messiah’s birthplace, of
Zechariah <b>9</b>:9 regarding his triumphal entry, of Zechariah <b>12</b>:10
with respect to his death, and of Psalm <b>16</b>:8-l1 for his resurrection. The
fact that a new covenant was to replace the Law of Moses is also brought to
light by the writer of Hebrews to show that the transition from the Law to the
gospel was prophesied in the Law itself.<span style='position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'>45</span> No clearer proof of continuity between new and old could
be imagined.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              The expression <i>must
be fulfilled</i> adds the certainty with which the prophecies of the Old
Testament were to be fulfilled in the New. Referring to Isaiah <b>53</b>:12,
Jesus declared, <i>“For I say to you, what is written must be fulfilled in me, ‘And
he was reckoned with the transgressors’: for what concerns me has fulfillment”</i>
(Luke <b>22</b>:37). After his resurrection he taught the apostles, <i>“These
are the words I spoke to you while I was yet with you, that all things must be
fulfilled that are written about me in the Law of Moses, the prophets, and the
psalms”</i> (Luke <b>24</b>:44). An emphasis on the certainty of fulfillment is
also an emphasis on the unity of Old Testament prediction and New Testament
fulfillment.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>46</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              Peter draws attention to
the inspiration of the prophets when he says that they, realizing there was a
meaning in their words, were not aware of what that meaning was:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-left:1.0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>“Concerning that salvation the prophets sought
diligently who prophesied the grace that would come to you, searching what time
or what kind of time the Spirit of Christ that was in them pointed to when he
testified beforehand about the sufferings of Christ and the glories that would
follow them. It was revealed to them that not to themselves, but unto you, they
ministered these things, which now have been announced to you through the one that
preached the gospel to you by the Holy Spirit sent forth from heaven, things angels
desire to look into”</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> (1 Peter <b>1</b>:10-12).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>To communicate a unified message when they were not
fully aware of its meaning, these men had to speak <i>“from God, moved by the
Holy Spirit.”</i><span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>47</span> The “Spirit
of Christ” was in the prophecy; Christ was in the fulfillment.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        d.   <u>Old Testament types
and New Testament antitypes</u>. In close relation to prophecy-fulfillment is
type-antitype. Their significance is the same<span style='position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'>48</span> although the form of presentation differs: prophecy comes
in verbal forms; type comes in in objective forms. Prophecy carries an
intentional meaning all its own, whereas type uses historic things, persons,
rituals, even words <i>per se </i>to “typify” beyond their first application. Preordained
representation is the essence of type. Predictive assertion is the essence of
prophecy. They are one in their forward thrust and in having an originally
intended meaning.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>49</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>                              </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>Differing as they do in the form of presentation, the
degree to which they were originally understood also differs. Though not always
aware of the meaning of their predictions,<span style='position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'>50 </span>the prophets were conscious that they were prophesying. But
types were concealed, not only as to their ultimate design, but also as to
whether they had a significance beyond their immediate representation. In
Hebrews <b>9</b> the author indicates that there was typical import to the
structure and contents of the tabernacle. He declares that the annual entrance
of the high priest into the Holy of Holies with blood offered for himself and
his people signified that the way into the holy place (heaven?) had not yet
been made manifest. That signification is attributed to the Holy Spirit. So in
a more subtle manner types indicate, as do prophecies, the inspiration of those
who recorded them.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>51</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>                        </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>e.  <u>Use of the term <i>scripture</i></u>. 2 Peter <b>3</b>:15-16
says,</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-left:1.0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
-31.5pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>          <i>“Our beloved brother Paul,
also according to the wisdom given to him, wrote to you; as also in all his
epistles, speaking of these things; wherein are some things hard to understand,
which the ignorant and unsteadfast twist, as they do also the other scriptures
to their own destruction.”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>The word <i>other</i> in connection with Paul’s claim
of inspiration classifies in one category (“scripture”) the writings of the Old
Testament and the epistles of Paul. The inspired character of the latter Peter
applies equally to the former.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>52</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>            </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>B.  Authority of the Old Testament</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  1.  Christ’s use of the Old
Testament</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.   <u>In self-defense</u>. Unity
within and between the two segments of God’s progressive revelation carried
with it the implication that the Hebrew scriptures were inspired. A second
argument to that same effect is the fact that Jesus the Christ, the Son of the
living God, used the canon of the Jews as his authority. In more than half the
times when Jesus defended his actions or his teachings, he used the scriptures
of the Old Testament. Sometimes he alluded to them only in the process of his
defense. When questioned about his eating with publicans and sinners, Jesus
replied that he had come to call these sinners to repentance, adding the
quotation from Hosea <b>6</b>:6a, <i>“I desire mercy more than sacrifice”</i>
(Matthew <b>9</b>:10-13). On other occasions he used the Old Testament as the
basis of his argument. On the resurrection Jesus countered the Sadducees by
referring to Exodus <b>3</b>:6. Perhaps the best illustration of Jesus’
attitude toward the Law comes from his defense against Satan himself in the wilderness.
He quoted without comment three times from Deuteronomy. In so doing he
demonstrated to all who read the account of his temptation that no greater
authority need be cited by any man as a bulwark against temptation. As a man
even the Son of God could rely on the inspiration of the scripture in such a
dark hour.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.   <u>In answer to Jewish
doctrinal questions</u>. In answering doctrinal questions about the Jewish
religion, Jesus rested his case on the authority of the Law, setting a
precedent for everyone in religious disputations. When asked by the Jews
whether the Mosaic system permitted divorce for any cause, he answered that it
was a temporary concession God made to hard-hearted men, but he added that God’s
original intention was one woman for one man, and cited the creation account in
verification (Matthew <b>19</b>:3-12).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  2.  Christians’ use of the Old
Testament</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.   <u>In self-defense</u>. The
apostle Peter, speaking before the elders of Israel, used Psalm<b> 118</b>:22
in his defense when arrested with John for healing the impotent man at Gate
Beautiful and for preaching the resurrection of Jesus Christ to the crowd who gathered
there (Acts.<b> 3</b>:10-11). Paul before Felix declared his belief in <i>“all
the things that are according to the Law and written in the prophets.” </i>Before
Herod Agrippa II, he said,</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-left:1.0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>“Having obtained God’s help, I stand to this day testifying
both to small and great, saying nothing but what the prophets and Moses said
would come: that the Christ must suffer and that he first by the resurrection would
proclaim light to the people and to the Gentiles”</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (Acts <b>26</b>:22-23).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>These men on trial for their lives sought refuge in
the authority of the Old Testament.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            Especially significant was the fact that
they did so by the guidance of the Holy Spirit according to one of the
permanent elements in the first commission to the Twelve:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-left:1.0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>“Behold, I send you out like sheep among wolves; be wise
as serpents and harmless as doves. But beware of people; they will deliver you
up to councils, and they will scourge you in their synagogues; yes, and you
will be brought before governors and kings for my sake for a testimony to them and
to the Gentiles. But when they deliver you up, do not worry how or what you will
