say, because it will be given you in that hour what to say. For it is not you
that speak but the Spirit of your Father that speaks in you. Brother will
deliver up brother to death, and the father his child; and children will rise
up against parents and cause them to be put to death.”</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>53 </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>                        </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>b.   <u>As a basis for their teaching</u>. That the Law
and the gospel have a common element of inspired truth appears in basing New
Testament doctrine on Old Testament teaching. Paul argues in Galatians <b>3</b>
that salvation is by faith. He uses Abraham (who lived before the Law was
given) as his example of a person who was not saved by meritorious works of law
(<b>3</b>:15-22). He shows from Habakkuk<b> 2</b>:4 that even those who lived
under the Mosaic economy were, in reality, saved by faith: <i>“the righteous
shall live by faith.”</i> James contends for salvation by obedience on the
basis of Abraham’s active response to divine command (James<b> 2</b>:21-23). Paul
founded his instruction about Christian stewardship on the passage from
Deuteronomy that an ox that treads out the grain should not be muzzled<span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'> </span>(Deuteronomy<b> 25</b>:4) and on
the Levitical principle that the priest at the altar should live of the altar
(1 Corinthians <b>9</b>:8-14). The place of women in the Lord’s work is
associated with the order of the creation and the fall in the Genesis record (1
Timothy<b> 2</b>:11-15). Only a law of divine origin is of value in presenting
a message that claims divine origin.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              Philip began with the “suffering
servant” poem of Isaiah<b> 53</b> and preached Christ to the Ethiopian eunuch. In
the two Sabbath-day sermons Paul delivered at Antioch of Pisidia, he proclaimed
the resurrection of Christ on the basis of Psalm <b>2</b>:7 and<b> 16</b>:10,
and the universal outreach of the gospel on Isaiah <b>44</b>:6 (Acts <b>13</b>:33-37,
46-48). The faithful Bereans are commended for their noble attitude because
they examined the scriptures daily to see <i>“whether these things were so.”</i>
Many of them therefore “believed”<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'> </span>(Acts
<b>17</b>:11).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            C.  Power of predictive prophecy</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  1.  Nature of the case</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.  <u>Involvements</u>. The
Holy Spirit is the agent of inspiration. Jesus promised his disciples that God
would send them another Comforter (John <b>14</b>:16), who would guide them
into all truth<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'> </span>(John <b>16</b>:13)
by quickening their memories, filling their minds (John <b>14</b>:26), and telling
them the things to come (John <b>16</b>:13). He would declare to them the
future, so as the agent of inspiration he is the agent of prophecy.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              Predictive prophecy is
not human prescience. The Old Testament prophets were not just smart men with
keen insight into the implications of current trends. The things they
prophesied were too minute in detail, too general in scope, too far in the
future to be foreseen in current events. The apostle Peter says: <i>“No
prophecy of scripture is of personal prognostication, because no prophecy ever
came by the will of man.”</i><span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>54</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.  <u>Consequence</u>. Any
assertion crediting an Old Testament writer with the power of predictive
prophecy implies the inspiration of that writer since people unaided
supernaturally cannot foretell the future: <i>“Men spoke from God, being moved
by the Holy Spirit” </i>(2 Peter<b> 1</b>:20b).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  2.   Phenomenology</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.   <u>Scope of the
prophecies</u>. Zechariah prophesied the spear thrust on Calvary 550 years
ahead of time.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>55</span> In the eighth
century B.C. a Jew prophesied the hope of salvation for Gentiles,<span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>56</span> a fact that Christ’s own
apostles were slow to see.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>57</span> Fourteen
hundred years before the coming of Christ, Moses stood among people without a
country and prophesied that from among them God would raise up his successor.<span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>58</span> Prophecies of such detail,
scope, and far-distant fulfillment must be the product of inspired men.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.  <u>Speakers of the
prophecies</u>. The New Testament cites some thirty predictive prophecies as
the words of Old Testament writers. Moses, David, Isaiah, Jeremiah, Daniel,
Joel, and Malachi<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>59</span> are cited
twenty-two times by name in such quotations as Matthew’s reference to Isaiah at
the beginning of the ministry of John the Baptist:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  <i>“For this is he that was spoken
of through the prophet, saying,</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        ‘The voice of one crying in
the wilderness,</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        “Make ready the way of the
Lord;</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        Make his paths straight.”’”</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>60</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>At other times the New Testament does not identify the
origin, as with the first prediction fulfilled in the New Testament:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-left:1.0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>“Now all this is come to pass that it might be
fulfilled which was spoken by the Lord through the prophet, saying,</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        ‘Behold a virgin will be with
child and </span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        she will bring forth a son,</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        and they will call him
Immanuel’”</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> [Isaiah <b>7</b>:14].</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>Referring to the person without exact identification
adds Micah and Asaph to the men endowed with the power of predictive prophecy.<span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>61</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>                  </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>What applies to the prophets also applies to what they
wrote. The New Testament quotes nearly fifty predictive prophecies without
mentioning authorship human or divine. Such a practice reveals first the
acceptance of the passages as prophecy, and secondly the inspiration of the men
who wrote them. Of special interest is Galatians <b>3</b>:8, where Paul not
only personifies scripture, but gives it the ability to prophesy:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-left:1.0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>“And the scripture, foreseeing that God would justify the
Gentiles by faith, preached the gospel beforehand to Abraham, saying, ‘All the nations
will be blessed in you.’”</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;position:
relative;top:-3.0pt'>62</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>Neither scripture nor the men who wrote it can foresee
except by the Spirit’s power to transcend temporal limitations.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            D.  Revelation as divine origin in origin</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  1.  Nature of the case</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.   <u>Distinction between
inspiration and revelation</u>. Revelation makes known what is not knowable by
the person who received it. The writer need not understand the meaning of the
message, because he has still been made aware of the facts themselves (cp. 1
Peter <b>1</b>:10-12). Revelation makes known; inspiration (“God-breathedness”)
emphasizes the source of revelation.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>63</span> Even
as <i>revelation</i> means the act of making known as well as to the fact made
known, so also <i>inspiration</i> emphasizes both the divine source of the
message and the state of the person who received it. <i>Revealed</i> speaks of
the message only; <i>inspired</i> (as an English term) speaks of both the
message and the person. The prophet’s state of inspiration enables him to
communicate to others without error or omission the facts he has received in
revelation. So, though revelation and inspiration apply to much common ground,
inspiration applies to communicating the message to the ones the agent of
inspiration wants to deliver it.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.   <u>The argument</u>. Any
passage said to be God’s words expresses revelation and implies inspiration
since a revelation given to one man and intended for others does not fulfill
its purpose if it is improperly communicated.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  2.  Examples</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.  <u>Where the original
passage was not God’s words</u>. Jesus answered the question about divorce by citing
the creation account:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-left:1.0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>“Have you not read that he who made them from the
beginning made them male and female, and said, ‘For this reason a man will
leave his father and mother and cling to his wife, and the two shall become one
flesh’”</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> [Matthew <b>19</b>:4-5]?</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>The passage is Genesis <b>2</b>:24-25. The last
speaker is Adam in <b>2</b>:23, and the next two verses come without any
indication of who is speaking.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>64 </span>A
reader might well suppose these verses were words that Moses injected at that
point. However, Jesus ascribes them to God as an answer to Adam’s previous
statement: <i>“This is now bone of my bones and flesh of my flesh; she will be
called woman because she was taken out of man.”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.   <u>Where the original
passage was God’s words</u>. Peter, rising to defend the charismatic phenomena
on Pentecost, quoted Joel <b>2</b>:28-32 as prophetic of that occasion. In the
declaration of Acts <b>2</b>:17, <i>“‘It will come to pass in the last days,’
says God,” </i>Joel’s prophecy does on include “<i>says God.</i>” Although the
speaker in Joel is expressly Yahveh (<b>2</b>:19), Peter, speaking under the
power prophesied by Joel, ascribes to God the words he quotes from “Joel’s”
prophecy.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>65, 66</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>III. Direct Assertion  </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <u>Direct assertion</u> means that no inferences
from the New Testament statements are needed to reach the claim in this essay. The
claim itself is stated: the Old Testament is the message of the Holy Spirit
written; it is inspired.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            A.  Specific scriptures attributing the
Old Testament to the Holy Spirit</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  1.  Inspiration and the Holy Spirit</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.  <u>The evidence</u>. Inspiration
is the supernatural influence present in the authoritative communication of a
divine message and active in direct assertions, judicious selections, and
accurate narrations.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>67</span> We
have already shown that the Holy Spirit is the agent of inspiration.<span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>68</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.  <u>The argument</u>. Directly
ascribing an Old Testament passage to the Holy Spirit, with or without mentioning
the human writer, states that writer’s inspiration.<span style='position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'>69</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  2.  Passages in point</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.   <u>Mentioning the human
writer</u>. As Peter addressed the assembly of apostles </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              about appointing someone
to replace Judas Iscariot, he said:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:31.5pt;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-31.5pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        <i>“Brothers, it was needful
that the scripture should be fulfilled which the Holy </i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:31.5pt;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-31.5pt'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        Spirit spoke before by David
concerning Judas:</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        ‘Let his habitation be made
desolate,</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        And let no man dwell therein’;</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  and</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        ‘Let someone else take his
office.’”</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>70</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>The Holy Spirit was the agent; David was the
instrument.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>71</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.  <u>Without mentioning the
writer</u>. The writer of Hebrews goes beyond the practice elsewhere in not
even specifying the human author:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  <i>“Wherefore, even as the Holy
Spirit says,</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        ‘Today if you hear his voice,</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        Do not harden your hearts as
in the provocation,</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        As in the day of the trial in
the wilderness; </span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        As I swore in my wrath,</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              “They will not enter
into my rest.”’”</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'>72</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>This scripture is from a psalm that has no indication
of authorship in its heading. The writer sees fit to document his citation as
the words of the Holy Spirit.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            B.  Scriptures attributing the Old
Testament to the Holy Spirit</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  1.   Examples with reference to persons</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.   <u>2 Peter <b>1</b>:16-21</u>.
In an appeal for his readers to remember the faith they possessed and to be
established in the truth of its message, Peter relied on the message of the
prophets made sure by the experience of himself and others. They had not
followed fables in presenting the power and presence of the Lord Jesus Christ. Peter
cited the certainty of his experience on the Mount of Transfiguration and the
inspiration of those who had foretold the sufferings and glory of Christ. These
ancient seers did not devise cunning fables, because prophecy in the scriptural
sense did not come because clever men decided to try to prophesy.  They were <i>“moved
by the Holy Spirit”</i> to prophesy, and their prophecies were fulfilled in the
presence of men like Peter. They who had hearkened to the prophets had now heard
the voice of God himself: <i>“This is my beloved Son.”</i><span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>73</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.  <u>1 Peter <b>1</b>:10-12</u>.
That the message of the prophets was not their own invention is evident in
their ignorance of its import, in their desire to understand its meaning, and
in their attempt to know the time and kind of time when it would be fulfilled. The
Spirit of Christ testified beforehand to the sufferings of Christ, and
announced afterward their significance through the messengers of the gospel. Both
those who prophesied and those who preached were guided by the same Spirit sent
forth from heaven.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  2.  Examples referring to the
written documents</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        a.  <u>2 Timothy <b>3</b>:16-17</u>.
At the outset of this essay, we gave reasons for identifying the first-century
canon with the Old Testament we have today.<span style='position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'>74</span> The argument also said that the New Testament witnesses
to a definite literary unit called the scripture.<span style='position:relative;
top:-3.0pt'>75</span> The exact books in that canon show up in New Testament
quotations, factual allusions, and verbal reminiscences from Old Testament
writings—except for three books that New Testament writers found no occasion to
cite: Ruth, Ezra, Lamentations.<span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>76</span> But
even these three are approved, being commonly attached to books that <u>are </u>cited.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                              On the background of that
evidence, the importance of Paul’s statement to Timothy becomes evident:  </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-left:1.0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>“Every scripture, inspired of God, is profitable for teaching,
reproof, correction, instruction which is in righteousness so that the man of
God may be complete, thoroughly furnished for every good work.”</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>77  </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>The King James Version translated the first part of
this quotation, <i>“All scripture is given by inspiration of God.” </i>The
different placement of <i>is</i> comes from its having to be supplied since it
is omitted in the Greek. The King James Version, by placing <i>is</i>
immediately after <i>scripture</i>, makes the inspiration of the Law a direct
affirmation. The arrangement of the American Standard is perhaps more powerful,
because it states the fact as a presupposition already settled without a need
for emphatic assertion.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            The two versions also differ in saying “all
scripture” and “every scripture.” Though <i>pasa</i> is singular and usually
translated “every,” “all” may be justified with proper nouns as seen in the
expression <i>all Judaea</i> in Matthew <b>3</b>:5; <i>scripture</i> may be
considered a proper noun. <i>Every scripture</i> particularizes what <i>all
scripture</i> generalizes; but whether general or particular, the sense is the
same: the Old Testament is inspired.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <i>“Inspired of God”</i> (ASV) and <i>“given
by inspiration of God”</i> (KJV) are loose translations of a passive verbal
whose exact meaning is “God-breathed.” <i>Inspired</i>, a word of Latin
derivation, is not strictly accurate, because it implies the wrong word
picture. The figure is not that God breathed into the already-existent writings
of the Old Testament. God “breathed” the writings themselves; he exhaled them,
so to speak. He did not adopt what was human in origin. He gave to men what was
from himself: the Old Testament is God-breathed.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        b.  <u>John <b>10</b>:34-36</u>.
Standing in front of a mob ready to stone him for blasphemy, the Son of God
made his defense from Psalm <b>82</b>:6:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-left:1.0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>“Is it not written in your Law, ‘I said, “You are Gods”’?
If he called them gods to whom the word of God came (and the scripture cannot
be broken), do you say of him whom the Father sanctified and sent into the
world, ‘You are blaspheming’ because I said, ‘I am the Son of God’?”</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-31.5pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>The expression <i>and the scripture cannot be broken</i>
is the basic element in what Jesus said. It is the authoritative fact from
which he concludes that his enemies err in trying to stone him because of the
term he used for himself.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        Reasoning from the general to
the particular, Jesus makes a statement of threefold importance. “The scripture”
is an identifiable, organic whole that has the attribute <i>“not able to be
broken.”</i> Secondly, <i>scripture</i> is a literary term as made obvious by
its fundamental meaning (“writing”), and by being distinguished from the
expression <i>word of God</i> preceding the parenthetical note. Thirdly, <i>the
scripture cannot be broken</i> declares the character of the whole Jewish canon,
because Jesus argues from general to particular: the authority of the whole
that Psalm <b>82</b>:6 pronounces includes this authoritative particular. We
could expect no stronger proof for the inspiration of the Old Testament than
this verdict of God’s own Son: <i>“the scripture cannot be broken.”</i><span
style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>78</span></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;
margin-bottom:0in;margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:center'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>Summary and Conclusion</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  I. Summary</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            A.  Silence</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  The silence of the New Testament on
such an important matter as the Jewish canon is presumptive evidence that their
canon was correct in the estimation of Christ.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            B.  Implication</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  The inspiration of the Old Testament
is implied in the New Testament’s usage of it as a literary unit, authoritative
whole, and prophetic document.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            C.  Assertion</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  In addition to the presumptive
argument from silence and the implication of unity, authority, and prophecy,
the case for the inspiration of the Old Testament is sealed by direct
assertions in the New Testament.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> II. Conclusion</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            A.  Certainty of the case</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  The New Testament evidence for the
inspiration of the Old Testament is quite conclusive. The difficulty is not
finding proof for the proposition but systematizing the amount of material into
a coherent presentation. The abundance, rather than the scarcity, of evidence
is the problem that the topic presents.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            B.  Areas for further study</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  1.  Insufficiency of quotation to
prove inspiration</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        Attempts have tried to weaken
the force of the evidence and confuse the points of proof that appear above. Of
importance in this respect is the fact that quotations, allusions, and
reminiscences of the Old Testament do not in themselves prove inspiration. In
response, we stress the <u>manner</u> in which these quotations are made, which
shows the reason they are cited.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  2.   Insignificance of the silence
of Jesus</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        Some have claimed that the
scope of Christ’s work would not have included a pronouncement of confirmation
or rejection on the official list of writings the scribes used in the synagogues.
That claim misunderstands the canon and the purpose of Jesus’ ministry. God
himself determined the canon, not the scribes in the synagogues. Evidence of that
basis for the canon can be presented, and the need for Christ’s pronouncement
on the Jewish canon can be demonstrated.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  3.  Accommodation</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        Others have charged that Jesus
accommodated himself to the current viewpoint, so that his statements may not
express his personal attitude toward the Old Testament. Whereas it is true in a
sense that he accommodated himself to the circumstances of incarnation, a study
of biblical accommodation makes plain that he did not accommodate himself to
the ignorance and errors of the day. The ethical implications of his so doing
can be shown from his own teachings to be a denial of his Messiahship.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  4.  <i>Kenosis</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        The doctrine of <i>kenosis</i>
also bears on Christ’s testimony to the canon. By over-emphasizing his “emptying”
in becoming incarnate, some have tried to deprive him of his deity. If the Son
of God lay by his glory to the extent that his testimony on the canon is
inconclusive, then his testimony on salvation would also be inconclusive. A
study of the scriptural idea in <i>kenosis</i> can offset that whole argument
against Christ’s witness to the inspiration of the Old Testament.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                  5.  Extent of inspiration</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>                        Not only does the New
Testament witness to the inspiration of the Old Testament, but it also
witnesses the extent of that inspiration. The Christian scriptures view the
Jewish scriptures as verbally inspired.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-right:-.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-right:-.5in'><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;
margin-bottom:0in;margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:center'><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>Endnotes</span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>1</span>In
his trial before the Sanhedrin, Stephen cites Amos <b>5</b>:25-27 under the
rubric <i>as it is written in the book of the prophets</i>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>2</span><b>John
10:34</b> (Psalm <b>82</b>:6); <b>12</b>:34; <b>15:25</b> (Psalm<b> 35</b>:19; <b>69</b>:4);
<b>1 Corinthians 14:21</b> (Isaiah <b>28</b>:11-12);<b> Galatians 3:11</b>
(Habakkuk<b> 2</b>:4).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>3</span>2
Timothy <b>3</b>:15 (</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt'>ἱερὰ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt'>γράμματα</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> [<i>hiera grammatica</i>]).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>4</span>Romans<b>
1</b>:2 (</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt'>γραφαὶ</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt'>ἅγιαι</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> [<i>graphai hagiai</i>]).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>5</span>The
Pentateuch comprised the first part; the rest of the books made up the second. The
expression occurs in the following passages: Luke <b>16</b>:29, 31; <b>24</b>:27;
Acts <b>26</b>:22; <b>28</b>:23. <i>Moses and the prophets</i> occurs only in Luke’s
writings.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>6</span>Matthew
<b>5</b>:17; <b>7</b>:12; <b>11</b>:13; 22:40; Luke 1<b>6</b>:16; Acts <b>13</b>:15
(?);<b> 24</b>:14; Romans <b>3</b>:21.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>7</span>Note
also that Christ’s expression in Luke <b>24</b>:44: <i>the Law of Moses and the
prophets and the Psalms</i> stands as a metonymy for the whole, and occurs
instead of the usual term <i>the writings </i>because it was the most important
member, especially since Jesus’ purpose in this resurrection appearance was to
teach the disciples all the things he fulfilled about himself.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>8</span>See
William Henry Green, <i>General Introduction to the Old Testament</i>. (New
York: Charles Scribner’s Sons, 1899), p. 208.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>9</span><i>Ibid</i>.,
p. 123.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>            10</span><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>Ibid</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>11</span><i>Ibid</i>.,
pp. 123-124.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>12</span>R.
C. Foster’s booklet <i>The Battle of the Versions</i>. (Cincinnati: Cincinnati
Christian University), p. 32.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            </span><span lang=ES style='font-size:
12.0pt;position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>13</span><i><span lang=ES
style='font-size:12.0pt'>Ibid</span></i><span lang=ES style='font-size:12.0pt'>.,
p. 33.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span lang=ES
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>14</span>Josephus,
<i>Contra Apionem</i> <b>1</b>:8</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>;</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>trans. William
Whiston. <i>The Complete Works of Flavius Josephus</i>. (Philadelphia: John E.
Potter and Company).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>15</span>William
Henry Green. <i>op. cit</i>., p. 126.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>16</span><i>Ibid</i>.,
p. 127.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>17</span>John
<b>2</b>:22; <b>7</b>:38, 42; <b>10</b>:35; <b>17</b>:12; <b>19</b>:28; Acts <b>1</b>:16;
<b>8</b>:32; Romans <b>4</b>:3; <b>9</b>:17; <b>10</b>:11; <b>11</b>:2;
Galatians <b>3</b>:8, 22; <b>4</b>:30; 2 Timothy 3<b>:16</b>; James<b> 2</b>:8;
<b>4</b>:5;<b> </b>1 Peter<b> 2</b>:6;<b> 2 </b>Peter <b>1</b>:20.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>18</span>Acts
<b>17</b>:2. See also Matthew<b> 21</b>:42;<b> 22</b>:29; <b>26</b>:54; Mark <b>14</b>:49;
Luke <b>24</b>:27, 32, 45; John <b>5</b>:39; Acts <b>17</b>:11;<b> 18</b>:24,
28; Romans<b> 1</b>:2; <b>15</b>:4; 1 Corinthians<b> 15</b>:3, 4. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>19</span>All
the usages of <i>scripture</i>, singular and plural, are accompanied by the
article except four: John <b>19</b>:37; 2 Timothy <b>3</b>:16; 1 Peter <b>2</b>:6;
2 Peter<b> 1</b>:20.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>20</span>1
Corinthians <b>14</b>:21. The other examples are John<b> 10</b>:34; <b>12</b>:34;
<b>15</b>:25.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>21</span>Luke
<b>16</b>:16. <i>The Law and the prophets </i>also occurs in Matthew<b> 5</b>:17;
<b>7</b>:12; <b>11</b>:13; <b>22</b>:40; John <b>1</b>:45; Acts<b> 14</b>:14; <b>28</b>:23;
Romans <b>3</b>:21.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>22</span>2
Chronicles<b> 24</b>:20-22. An obvious corruption of the text occurs in
Matthew’s account at this point since Zechariah “son of Berechiah” would
indicate one of the minor prophets (Zechariah <b>1</b>:1), whose manner of
death we do not know. Luke’s parallel account (<b>11</b>:50) omits <i>son of
Berechiah</i>. The text of Matthew in Codex Sinaiticus firsthand omits the
phrase, as do some ancient evangelisteria and Eusebius (twice). Originally,
Matthew probably read <i>“from the blood of Abel the righteous unto the blood
of Zechariah, whom you slew between the sanctuary and the altar.”</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>23</span>It
is beside the point that the books of scripture may have stood separately,
rather than together in a continuous scroll, because literary arrangement comes
from a foregone conclusion. Of more importance is the consideration that Jesus
could have purposely chosen the martyrdom of Zechariah because it illustrated
so well the godlessness of men that would murder a prophet of God in the
precincts of God’s temple of God; Jesus was standing in the temple when he
spoke. He had just mentioned the temple and the altar in this denunciation of
the scribes and Pharisees (vv. 16-21). The Pharisees had tried to stone Jesus
himself in the temple area (John <b>8</b>:59;<b> 10</b>:31). Nonetheless, the
possible implications of his statement <i>“<u>from</u> . . . <u>Abel unto</u> .
. . <u>Zechariah</u>”</i> are worthy of note. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>24</span>George
L. Robinson, “Canon of the Old Testament,” <i>The International Standard Bible
Encyclopaedia</i> (Grand Rapids: Wm. B. Eerdmans Publishing Company, 1939) I,
561.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>25</span>Donald
Guthrie, <i>Hebrews to Revelation</i> (Vol. III of <i>New Testament
Introduction</i>. 3 vols.; London: The Tyndale Press, 1966), p. 239.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>26</span><i>Ibid</i>.,
p. 238.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>27</span>For
a brief discussion of views concerning Jude and apocryphal writings, see Henry
Alford’s <i>The Greek New Testament</i> (New York: Harper &amp; Brothers,
Publishers, 1859), IV, 194-98.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>28</span>Cp.
John <b>4</b>:21, 23-24; Matthew <b>26</b>:61 (John<b> 2</b>:19-21); Matthew<b>
27</b>:40; and Acts <b>6</b>:14.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>29</span>Examples
are healing on the Sabbath (Matthew <b>12</b>:9-14; Mark. <b>2</b>:1-12; Luke <b>6</b>:6-11;
<b>13</b>:10-17; <b>14</b>:1-6; John <b>5</b>:1-16; <b>7</b>:19-24); eating
with publicans and sinners (Matthew <b>9</b>:10-13; Mark <b>2</b>:15-17); “harvesting”
on the Sabbath (Matthew <b>12</b>:1-8; Mark <b>2</b>:23-28; Luke <b>6</b>:1-5);
eating with unwashed hands (Matthew<b> 15</b>:1-20; Mark <b>7</b>:1-23; Luke <b>11</b>:37-41).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>30</span>Examples
are cleansing the temple (Matthew<b> 21</b>:23-32; Mark <b>11</b>:27-33; Luke <b>20</b>:1-8;
John <b>2</b>:13-22) and letting people praise him after the triumphal entry
(Luke <b>19</b>:37-40).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>31</span>“Obvious
quotations” means passages that, by formula or other means, introduce Old
Testament passages recognizably as quotations even by someone not familiar with
the language of the Law. “Factual allusion” refers to events, persons, or
things without using the wording of the original. “Verbal reminiscences” are
expressions in the Old Testament.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>32</span>See
1 Peter <b>2</b>:5-6. <i>Scripture</i> here is anarthrous, directing attention
to its nature, rather than to its phenomenology.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>33</span></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt'>Γέγραπται</span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>[<i>gegraptai</i>]
occurs in Matthew <b>4</b>:4, 6, 7, 10; <b>11</b>:10; <b>21</b>:13; <b>26</b>:24,
31; Mark <b>1</b>:2; <b>7</b>:6; <b>9</b>:12, 13; <b>11</b>:17; <b>14</b>:21,
27; Luke<b> 2</b>:23;<b> 3</b>:4;<b> 4</b>:4, 8, 10, 17;<b> 7</b>:27; <b>10</b>:26;
<b>19</b>:46; <b>20</b>:17; <b>24</b>:46; John <b>8</b>:17; Acts <b>1</b>:20; <b>7</b>:42;
<b>13</b>:33; <b>15</b>:15; and <b>23</b>:5. See also the next endnote.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>34</span>Luke
<b>22</b>:37; John <b>2</b>:17; <b>6</b>:31, 45; <b>8</b>:17; <b>10</b>:34; <b>12</b>:14,
16; <b>15</b>:25; Acts <b>13</b>:29; <b>24</b>:14; 1 Corinthians <b>15</b>:54;
2 Corinthians <b>3</b>:7;<b> 4</b>:13; and Galatians<b> 3</b>:10. Compare </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt'>λέγω</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:
12.0pt'> </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>in Acts <b>13</b>:34, 40.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>35</span>The
passages are Psalm<b> 2</b>:7; Deuteronomy <b>32</b>:43 (LXX; cf. Psalm<b> 97</b>:7);
Psalm <b>104</b>:4; <b>45</b>:6-7; <b>102</b>:25-27; and <b>110</b>:1. Note
also Hebrews <b>2</b>:12-13.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>36</span>Cp.
Luke<b> 20</b>:42 and Acts<b> 1</b>:20.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>37</span>The
Book of Psalms contains contributions from David (Psalm <b>1</b>), the sons of
Korah (Psalm <b>44</b>), Asaph (Psalm <b>50</b>), Solomon (Psalm <b>72</b>),
Heman (Psalm <b>88</b>), Etham (Psalm <b>89</b>), Moses (Psalm <b>90</b>), and
others not named. They were written during the nine centuries in which these
authors lived. Their time for composition nearly equals that of the whole Old
Testament.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>38</span>A
rest for God’s people is not only an inference from God’s resting; Psalm<b> 95</b>:11
asserts that there is a Sabbath rest for God’s people. The Hebrew writer
connects the two facts already revealed, showing that the first implies the second.
Although the human rest is directly promised in Psalm<b> 115</b>:11, Hebrews<b>
4</b>:10 shows that the writer also draws that fact independently from the
divine rest itself. It is the writer’s attitude toward the Old Testament that
is at point here rather than whether the Old Testament also directly affirms in
another place what was inferred from Genesis <b>2</b>:2.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>39</span>Note
that Psalm <b>95</b> as a Davidic Psalm is not known apart from Hebrews <b>4</b>:7;
so, the four hundred years is a loose number for the time between the Mosaic
authorship of the Pentateuch and the Davidic authorship of Psalm<b> 95</b>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>40</span>In
Antioch of Pisidia also, Paul shows the Messianic thrust of Jewish history.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>41</span>Brooke
Foss Westcott and Fenton John Anthony Hort, <i>The New Testament in the
Original Greek</i>. (New York: The Macmillan Company, 1957), pp. 601-18.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>42</span>John
<b>5</b>:39; note also Acts <b>24</b>:14.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>43</span>Galatians
<b>1</b>:11-12. For other claims and accounts of inspiration, see Acts<b> 2</b>:4;
<b>7</b>:29; 1 Corinthians <b>7</b>:40; 1 Thessalonians<b> 4</b>:9; Revelation <b>22</b>:17-20;
1 Peter <b>1</b>:12. To these we may add the indirect claims by the apostles
who wrote Gospels (Matthew, Luke, and John) and recorded the promises to
apostles (which included themselves): Matthew <b>10</b>:20; (Luke <b>24</b>:48);
John <b>14</b>:26; <b>16</b>:7-15; Acts<b> 1</b>:4-8.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>44</span>See
Romans<b> 10</b>:4; Acts <b>22</b>:14-15; and Paul’s statement before Herod
Agrippa II in Acts <b>26</b>:22-23.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>45</span>Jeremiah
<b>31</b>:31-35, cited in Hebrews <b>8</b>:8-12.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>46</span>Take
care not to understand the phrase <i>must be fulfilled</i> as indicating some kind
or degree of determinism. That determinism is not the point is evident in
Matthew <b>26</b>:53-54, where Jesus forgoes using his power to deliver himself
from enemies who were about to kill him: <i>“Or do you think that I cannot ask
my Father, and he will even now send me more than twelve legions of angels? How
then would the scriptures be fulfilled, that thus it must be?”</i> Other
passages where <i>must</i> emphasizes the certainty of fulfillment are Matthew <b>17</b>:10
(= Mark<b> 9</b>:11); Acts<b> 1</b>:16; <b>17</b>:3.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>47</span>2
Peter <b>1</b>:21. 1 Peter <b>1</b>:10-12 also asserts inspiration since the
Holy Spirit provides the message. However, it affords a line of implied
evidence because the prophets themselves did not know the fulfillment of their
own prophecies.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>48</span>That
no difference exists between type and prophecy is evident from the use of the
formula <i>in order that the scripture might be fulfilled</i> for both forms of
prediction.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>49</span>The
meaning of a type is intended by the writer, not added later by the
interpreter.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>50</span>See
endnote 47 and the discussion it is attached to.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>51</span>Other
examples of types abound, including such “verbal” types as perhaps Matthew <b>2</b>:15,
<i>“Out of Egypt have I called my son” </i>(Hosea <b>11</b>:1); Matthew<b> 2</b>:17,
<i>“A voice was heard in Ramah, weeping and great mourning, Rachel weeping for
her children, and she would not be comforted, because they were not”</i>
(Jeremiah <b>31</b>:15); Matthew<b> 2</b>:23, <i>“he should be called a
‘Nazarene’”</i> (Isaiah <b>11</b>:1 in the Hebrew); Hebrews <b>1</b>:5<i>, “I
will be to him a Father, and he shall be to me a Son?”</i> (2 Samuel <b>7</b>:14).
The Hosea reference has a different connection with Matthew’s point since Jesus
was Messiah for, and part of, “Israel,” which Hosea calls a son at Israel’s
exodus. Jesus returned to Palestine to fulfill his role among his people rather
than grow up in Egypt and carry on his role there. 2 Samuel<b> 7</b>:14 does
not make Solomon a type of Christ. “Seed” is an inclusive term for David’s whole
lineage. Solomon as one part of the seed built the temple, but Jesus as the
later Son was the seed the Hebrews is talking about.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>52</span>Cp.
1 Timothy<b> 5</b>:18, where Paul combines under the term <i>scripture</i> a
passage from Deuteronomy <b>25</b>:4 and a proverb of Christ in Matthew <b>10</b>:10
and Luke <b>10</b>:7, which existed in these written accounts by the time Paul
authored 1 Timothy.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>53</span>Matthew<b>
10</b>:16-21. That this element was a permanent one is evident in<b> 10</b>:22<i>:
“he that endures to the end, the same shall be saved.”</i> Note also that in
Peter’s defense before the Sanhedrin, Luke records, <i>“Then Peter, filled with
the Holy Spirit, said to them . . .”</i> With reference to Paul the same thing
is said in Acts<b> 13</b>:9.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>54</span>2
Peter <b>1</b>:20, 21a. The American Standard and King James Versions read at
this point: <i>“no prophecy of scripture is of (any) ‘private interpretation .
. .’”</i> The marginal reading is “special” interpretation. Peter is not saying
that people have no right to read the Bible for themselves, because (1) he is
talking about the <u>origination</u> of a prophecy [(1) cp. <b>1</b>:21, (2) </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt'>ἰδίας</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:
12.0pt'> </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt'>ἐπιλύσεως</span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>” [<i>idias epilyseōs</i>] = genitive of source, (3) </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt'>γίνεται</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>
[<i>ginetai</i>] ≠ </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt'>ἔστιν</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>[<i>estin</i>],
not the attempt to understand one already in existence. That Peter is talking
about the <u>origination</u> of prophecy is clear from the word <i>came</i> in
the next verse: <i>“for no prophecy ever <u>came</u>. . . .”</i> (2) The word <i>for</i>
indicates the reason that it is not of <i>“private interpretation”</i>: it did
not originate by human will; hence, <i>not of private interpretation</i> equals
<i>not by human will</i>, and both expressions refer to the <u>origin</u> of
prophecy. (3) What is “interpreted” is not the prophecy in scripture, but the
trends of history, whose uncertain implications are the only source that people
unaided supernaturally can use to predict the future. People’s vague guesswork
Peter denies is the nature of <i>“prophecies of scripture,”</i> that is, <i>“prophecies
in the scripture,”</i> or <i>“scriptural prophecies.”</i> People cannot
predictively prophesy in the <i>“scriptural”</i> sense by just deciding to (<i>“came
by will of man”</i>); they have no way to know what the future holds. They can only
do the next closest thing: prognosticate, the other kind of prediction.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>55</span>Zechariah
<b>12</b>:10, quoted in John <b>19</b>:37.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>56</span>Isaiah
<b>42</b>:1-4 (Matthew<b> 12</b>:18-21); <b>49</b>:6 (Acts <b>13</b>:47).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>57</span>Acts
<b>10</b>:9-23, 34-35; <b>11</b>:1-18; <b>15</b>:1-6, 7-11.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>58</span>Deuteronomy<b>
18</b>:15, 18 (Acts<b> 3</b>:22; <b>7</b>:37; John <b>1</b>:21, 25).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>59</span>Not
listing Zechariah here as one named in citations of his words supposes that the
text of Matthew <b>27</b>:9 possesses integrity. That passage names Jeremiah as
the author of Zechariah <b>11</b>:12-13. On details see F. C. Cook’s treatment
in <i>The Bible Commentary </i>of Matthew <b>27</b>:9, New Testament Vol. I,
pp. 189-90.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>            <span style='position:relative;top:-3.0pt'>60</span>Matthew
<b>3</b>:3; the other personal references to Isaiah are <b>Matthew 3:3</b> (<b>40</b>:4);
